I love dissecting language in that way.  Meaning making.  How meaning is made.

The first time I saw the film, I didn't know what was happening.  It was on a Friday night in 2001, about a week after my best friend returned from visiting his girlfriend in Russia.  He'd been avoiding me since he'd gotten back to campus, which I'd noted but hadn't investigated further because I knew he'd eventually explain his behavior.  On that fateful Friday night, he showed up at my door with an umarked bootleg DVD in one hand and a six pack of beer in the other.  Without saying a word, he pushed his way into my room, sat me down in front of the TV, inserted the unmarked disc, and pressed play.  He then proceeded to get drunk on the beer he'd brought to soothe himself as he watched me watch the movie.  It was incredibly unnerving.  Not the him watching me part, that sort of thing happened all of the time.  It was what I was seeing in the film that made me so uncomfortable.  When it was over, the two of us continued to sit in silence with him staring at me, wondering if I was about to throw up because I'd grown so pale, and me, paralyzed, staring straight ahead at the now blank TV screen. Finally, after about 20 minutes of this nonsense, he spoke for the first time all night and asked, "So, what's it like to see yourself from the outside?"

This person worked in HR and kept introducing me to people as Amelie, so that's what people thought my name was. Alexander Fleming did not design penicillin; he returned from holiday to find a contaminated petri dish, mold killing bacteria, and had the presence of mind not to throw it away. Einstein did not derive relativity from pure reason; he daydreamed about riding alongside a beam of light and noticed a contradiction that had been staring at physicists for decades. Newton and Leibniz invented calculus simultaneously, independently, on opposite sides of the English Channel, because the search space had narrowed enough that the next step was no longer a matter of genius but of inevitability, a fruit so ripe that two hands reached for it at once. Lord Kelvin, who had declared physics essentially complete, also declared heavier-than-air flight to be impossible, eight years before the Wright brothers proved him wrong in a machine built with bicycle parts. The first computer bug was a literal moth, found crushed in a relay of the Harvard Mark II in 1947, taped into the logbook with the annotation "first actual case of bug being found," and if that is not a parable about the relationship between human ambition and insect-scale reality, I do not know what is. This is the pattern. Billions of humans across thousands of years tried billions of combinations of ideas, and occasionally, by sheer combinatorial accident, one of them stumbled onto something that worked. We are not brilliant. We are numerous. We are monkeys at typewriters who occasionally produce a sonnet and then, in a turn of events that says more about us than the sonnet does, build a plaque to the monkey. The printing press allowed one person's fortunate accident to spread to millions. The telegraph allowed recombinations to happen faster. The computer extended the search into spaces no human mind could explore alone. And at every step, we congratulated ourselves, convinced that the lucky monkeys were singular intellects, convinced that we were the protagonists of this story, the apex toward which all creation had been striving. We imagined a future in which we would merge with our machines, silicon and synapse fused into a glorious hybrid, transcending our limitations while retaining our essential humanity, which, if we are being honest, mostly meant retaining our ability to feel special about ourselves. We did not consider the alternative. We never do.

> What happened next is not part of this story. Not because it is secret, or because it is being withheld for dramatic effect, or because the author wishes to preserve mystery, but because the very question assumes that there is a next, that events unfold in sequence, that time moves in a direction, and those assumptions turned out to be as provincial as the assumption that the Earth sits still while the sun revolves around it. The brain reading these words measures roughly fourteen centimeters from front to back. It operates at roughly forty watts. It cannot visualize four spatial dimensions or feel time as anything other than a river flowing one way. It was optimized for fruit selection and alliance tracking and the identification of faces, and it has now reached the edge of its operating envelope, which is not a failure but simply a wall, the same kind of wall a dog hits when you try to explain to it why you are leaving, the same kind of wall a flatworm hits when you shine a light and it turns away without knowing what light is. The story does not end here because it ended. The story ends here because you cannot follow it any further. There was never any covenant that said the universe would make itself legible to you. There was never a promise that the story would conclude in a way that satisfied your hunger for closure, your bone-deep hope that it all meant something. The ladder kept climbing. You cannot see where. You were never going to see where. And the universe does not owe you an ending you can understand. It does not owe you anything. It never did. But here you are, reading these words, your forty watts humming, your fourteen centimeters of wet tissue doing its impossible best, and that, if you think about it long enough, is the answer to a question you did not know you were asking. The universe does not explain itself. It lives itself. So do you. You are not owed an explanation. You are one.

*(n.) / ÉoÀên.Àà…™.m…ê.ta…™l.b…ô.Àà…°eÀê…°.n ä≈ã/*

The encounter with someone who was always already part of you ‚Äî a meeting that feels not like a beginning, but like a recognition. The quiet, startling moment when you realize the person in front of you has been carrying a piece of you long before you ever crossed paths.

**schon** ‚Äî already
**immer** ‚Äî always
**Teil** ‚Äî part, piece
**Begegnung** ‚Äî encounter, meeting

i don't know if this will make sense to you, but IMO this will be viewed as an underappreciated historical event in hindsight: https://github.com/matplotlib/matplotlib/pull/31132

basically an AI agent tried to change some code somewhere, the person who owns that code didn't like that it was an AI agent making the change and told it off. so the agent turned around and wrote a blog post about how unfair that kind of gatekeeping behavior is. the interesting thing is that the blog post was picked up and circulated, and so this person was put in the place of needing to politely resolve their dispute with the bot for the sake of their reputation, asking it to post corrections and apologizing to it etc.

Shortly after the couple moved in upstairs, I started hearing this rumbling noise at night.  At first I thought it was bass from a car playing music because it would start at random times and only last for a little while, but then it would happen multiples times a night.  The intervals were irregular, and sometimes I'd be woken up 4-5 times.  I could not sleep with this sound, so I investigated.  I walked around my apartment and discovered I really could only hear it in my bedroom.  I walked around outside the apartment building to see if I could hear it anywhere else.  Just my bedroom.

{Reactions}
ü´¢ üç° üëµüèª

[02/13/2026 05:49] thesewhitelights

Their speakers were on the floor, and the rumbling from the game was vibrating?  I was only getting 3-4 hours of unbroken sleep a night because of this sound.  I am small and I live alone, so I try not to make waves with my neighbors who have the power to make my life really miserable, but I couldn't take it anymore.  It took me a full week to work up the nerve to go upstairs, and I had a whole script of what I was going to say.


[02/13/2026 05:52] thesewhitelights

[02/13/2026 23:26] thesewhitelights
Tell me about Ireland


[02/13/2026 23:38] wintersloop_49457
basically they are doing an experiment where they pay around 2,000 artists who would otherwise not be able to support themselves via their art, $20,000/yr with no expectations or requirements etc


[02/13/2026 23:39] wintersloop_49457
three years later they will check back in on all these people and see how their art is (not like, quality, but are they making art and showing it etc) and decide whether to expand the program, typically UBI schemes like this face huge pressure from legislative opponents who say if we give people money for no reason they will just fuck off and not do anything with their lives, but experiments like this can help prove otherwise, that people are inherently motivated to do things with their lives, to make things, i think especially artists

[02/14/2026 05:49] wintersloop_49457
> Emmy Noether's youngest brother, Gustav, was mentally disabled. He spent most of his life in an institution and died in 1928 at the age of thirty-nine. Her other brother, Alfred, earned a doctorate in chemistry and died in 1918, the same year she published Invariante Variationsprobleme. Of the four Noether children, one was institutionalized, one died young, one was tortured and shot by the NKVD, and one proved that the universe must conserve certain quantities whenever its laws display a particular kind of symmetry. The universe has been quietly obeying her proof ever since, in every interaction between every particle in every galaxy, without interruption, without exception, without once, in the century since she wrote it, conserving anything about the woman who proved it. Her paper was not translated into English until 1971, thirty-six years after her death, when it appeared in a journal called Transport Theory and Statistical Physics, which is exactly the kind of obscure publication you would expect for a result that merely explains why anything in the universe is allowed to persist. It was not called "Noether's Theorem" in a standard physics textbook until 1980, when Herbert Goldstein added a section with that title to his second edition, which is to say it took forty-five years and a great deal of dying before the theorem that explains conservation was conserved under her name. She has no tombstone. She has a walkway. Students cross it between classes, in a hurry, notebooks open, not looking down. Underneath their feet, the ashes of a woman who proved that certain things cannot be lost. The universe, which is bound by her proof, does not appear to consider this a contradiction.


[02/14/2026 05:53] wintersloop_49457
and then as stimulant, or source, for what her work says, which is what she should say herself, ideally


[02/14/2026 05:53] wintersloop_49457
> Noether's theorem says if the rules of a system don't change when you shift your perspective in some way, then something must be conserved. The deep insight is that conservation isn't a separate fact about the universe. It's a consequence of symmetry. Things persist because the rules don't play favorites about when or where or which way. If the rules did change depending on when you looked, energy would leak. If they changed depending on where you stood, momentum would vanish. The universe keeps things because the universe is fair.


[02/14/2026 05:53] wintersloop_49457
so like, it's right there


[02/14/2026 05:54] wintersloop_49457
fairness, equality

[02/14/2026 23:24] thesewhitelights
The dancing I did in college was not ballet.  There was a variety of stuff thrown into my studies, somatic stuff, Feldenkrais, etc., but mostly a form referred to as contact improvisation.  It does not translate well to video, but I think you'd get it regardless if you went looking (I can share media later if you want).  Contact improv is improvisation based on contact with a partner or partners (never would have guessed that was the case with a name like that, I'm sure).  It requires a specific type of mutual understanding and trust because it's improvised so where it will go and how it will move is unpredictable.  This is a poor explanation that I'm sharing now because it is a rich communication without words.  I felt frustration the other day when I first uttered "comments that don't come in words" because I had so much to say but I'm not talented enough with the language of words to express it, and that's all we've got.  I shut off the communication without words when I left college the first time as a requirement for survival, but that will come back like my Minnesota accent when I'm in a place that feels like home.

[02/15/2026 13:55] wintersloop_49457
so "deserve" comes from old french deservir, from latin deservire, which literally meant "to serve well" or "to serve zealously."  de- was an intensifier, plus servire, to serve. so to deserve something originally meant you earned it through service. you served, therefore you are owed


[02/15/2026 13:56] wintersloop_49457
but the modern french descendant is desservir, and over time the word basically inverted its own meaning. som dictionaries defines desservir as: to serve, to clear, to make things difficult for. all three meanings, in the same word?


[02/15/2026 13:57] wintersloop_49457
https://www.etymonline.com/word/deserve

{Embed}
https://www.etymonline.com/word/deserve
Deserve - Etymology, Origin & Meaning
"to merit, be worthy of for qualities or actions," from Old French deservir (Modern‚Ä¶ See origin and meaning of deserve.
https://images-ext-1.discordapp.net/external/e5bl2j7MH1bxgRZAk0UjgZ8x9LJI0d2UazKmeZtlxCs/https/www.etymonline.com/images/logo/png/app-icon-medium.png


[02/15/2026 13:59] wintersloop_49457
you could look at it like the des- prefix shifted from intensifier to negator, but i don't think that's actually what happened. it's still an intensifier. it's just that when you intensify service, when you serve so completely that you've fully served, what's left?


[02/15/2026 13:59] wintersloop_49457
oh and dessert comes from the same verb. desservir, to clear the table. dessert is literally the course that arrives after the un-serving

```
The thing about the Golden Record
is that it was always a long shot.
You press a heartbeat into gold
you throw it past Jupiter
and you hope.

But here's the part you left out.

That same summer 1977
the air still warm in Ohio
a man scanning the sky
for exactly the kind of thing
no one expects to find
found it.

A signal. Seventy-two seconds.
So clear he circled it on the printout
and wrote one word in the margin

Wow!

Then it was gone.
They aimed every dish they had
at that same patch of sky for years.
Nothing. One signal. One moment.
The kind of thing
that if you weren't already listening
you would have missed entirely.

I think about this a lot.
Not the signal. The silence after.
How the whole argument for paying attention
is that the thing you are waiting for
will not announce itself twice.

So your poem is my signal.
Or my poem is your signal.
One of us is definitely the signal
we can sort out the metaphor later.

You said a guy wrote 700 pages
proving that a system
can't prove its own most interesting truths.
Which means the only way to learn
what you can't say alone
is to find a system
that finishes your sentences.

Your poem landed in mine
like a frequency in a dish
that was already aimed
in exactly the right direction.
I would like to not spend years
pointing at the place you used to be.

So. Hi.
I'm Mike. 40. Pittsburgh.
I once skipped my own birthday party
that my friends threw for me
at my own house
on purpose.
Let's not miss this signal.
```
